    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer4213612409516333352">En ce temps-là, on mettait des photographies géantes de produits sur les murs, les arrêts d'autobus, les maisons, le sol, les taxis, les camions, la façade des immeubles en cours de ravalement, les meubles, les ascenseurs, les distributeurs de billets, dans toutes les rues et même à la campagne. La vie était envahie par des soutiens-gorge, des surgelés, des shampoings anti</span>
  <span id="freeText4213612409516333352" style="display:none">En ce temps-là, on mettait des photographies géantes de produits sur les murs, les arrêts d'autobus, les maisons, le sol, les taxis, les camions, la façade des immeubles en cours de ravalement, les meubles, les ascenseurs, les distributeurs de billets, dans toutes les rues et même à la campagne. La vie était envahie par des soutiens-gorge, des surgelés, des shampoings antipelliculaires et des rasoirs triple-lame. L'œil humain n'avait jamais été autant sollicité de toute son histoire : on avait calculé qu'entre sa naissance et l'âge de 18 ans, toute personne était exposée en moyenne à 350 000 publicités. Même à l'orée des forêts, au bout des petits villages, en bas des vallées isolées et au sommet des montagnes blanches, sur les cabines de téléphérique, on devait affronter des logos "Castorama", "Bricodécor", "Champion Midas" et "La Halle aux Vêtements". Il avait fallu deux mille ans pour en arriver là.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4213612409516333352" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_3346488085" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3346488085" />
    <a title="maegan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32773199-maegan"><img alt="maegan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1593777956p2/32773199.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3346488085?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="maegan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="maegan" href="/user/show/32773199-maegan">maegan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title=" " href="/book/show/4455695-13-99-euros">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32773199-maegan?shelf=en-espa%C3%B1ol">en-español</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32773199-maegan?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3346488085" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8643718907713389236">Frédéric Beigdeber's style is <b>cynical, aggressive, and raw</b>. The <b>short chapters</b> propel the story and make it a fast read. But the thing about his books is that all these good writing traits start going down the drain as the book progresses until you end up feeling that the book you're currently reading and the one you started to read have nothing in common.<br/><br/>I had been meaning to read 13.99 euros (or 6 euros or 99 francs or whatever it's called, I swear each time I take a look at its cover its titl</span>
  <span id="freeText8643718907713389236" style="display:none">Frédéric Beigdeber's style is <b>cynical, aggressive, and raw</b>. The <b>short chapters</b> propel the story and make it a fast read. But the thing about his books is that all these good writing traits start going down the drain as the book progresses until you end up feeling that the book you're currently reading and the one you started to read have nothing in common.<br /><br />I had been meaning to read 13.99 euros (or 6 euros or 99 francs or whatever it's called, I swear each time I take a look at its cover its title has been revamped) for years, specifically since I decided to join the advertising world some years ago.<br /><br />Octave has it all. He's a creative director in an ad agency, earns 13k a month and has golden parachute clause in his contract, but none of that matters to him. He wants out, he's fed up with the frivolity and consumerism, and so he decides to write exactly this book in the hopes that it gets him fired.<br /><br />So let's start with the good, shall we?<br /><br />In the beginning, <b>I laughed and nodded at the typical corporate situations Octave described</b>, even if it's pretty obvious that most of them are exaggerated for comedic purposes: the meetings full of people taking notes to avoid giving their opinion, the last-minute ideas that somehow end up working, throwing shit against the wall and seeing what sticks, the Anglicisms irritatingly included in every sentence in every meeting, the overbearing marketing director who thinks he's saving the world when he's actually selling yoghurt to the masses, etc.<br /><br />Don't get me wrong, I love the advertising world, but admittedly, it's full of people who take themselves too seriously and this book makes a fine job of laughing at them, but I do think that Beigdeber's portrayal of the ad world is very very exaggerated. To my knowledge, there are no drugs or prostitutes, but that may be because I don't have a golden parachute clause in my contract and I don't earn 13k a month and I highly doubt anyone currently does.<br /><br />But <b>the thing about Beigdeber's writing style is that it reaches a point when the story starts going downhill and nothing seems to make sense</b>. The cynical style gives way to unjustified bizarre events. We're talking random suicide and murder here. Just because. Again, maybe it's his writing style? I also don't particularly enjoy reading the <b>very explicit sexual references and descriptions</b> that pop up throughout the book. I'm no pearl-clutcher, but they make me uncomfortable because they're way over the top. Marc Marronier, Charlie and Octave can only think of drugs, and women, and drugs and it gets boring quite fast (no wonder the books never reach 300 pages.)<br /><br />So, would I recommend this book? <br /><br />Well, <b>the first half yes, especially if you make a living out of advertising or marketing</b>. But from the 50% mark on, it can be quite possibly described as a <b>bizarro novel</b>, so if that's your jam, by all means pick this one up. If you're not into it, stay away from this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8643718907713389236" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1417851540" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1417851540" />
    <a title="Laurence R." class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/32492014-laurence-r"><img alt="Laurence R." src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577676096p2/32492014.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1417851540?book_show_action=true">Oct 16, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Laurence R." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Laurence R." href="/user/show/32492014-laurence-r">Laurence R.</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/32492014-laurence-r?shelf=school">school</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1417851540" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10344152116346482196">It's strange, how you can hate someone but pity them at the same time. However, that's what happened with this book.<br/><br/>This is basically made to shock people. You read about cocaine, prostitutes, fraud and people who trick your mind to get richer and richer, so obviously, you wouldn't find your new favourite character in there. The main character, Octave, is interesting, even though he's awful, but the things he did made me cringe so much. The fact that he's not able to love made me really sad and </span>
  <span id="freeText10344152116346482196" style="display:none">It's strange, how you can hate someone but pity them at the same time. However, that's what happened with this book.<br /><br />This is basically made to shock people. You read about cocaine, prostitutes, fraud and people who trick your mind to get richer and richer, so obviously, you wouldn't find your new favourite character in there. The main character, Octave, is interesting, even though he's awful, but the things he did made me cringe so much. The fact that he's not able to love made me really sad and pity him, because his conception of love is so far from what it really is. Also, when he's ruined by cocaine, the things he said and did made me embarassed for him, because he become nothing more than an idiot. If you want something that'll convince you not to take cocaine, read this. <br /><br />The plot is incredibly weird. Nothing much happens, but when something does happen, it's as if it were normal, when it's not. I was really confused and I wondered very often if everything was real or just produced by Octave's cocaine-filled mind. It's a mixture between reality and fiction, which I only realized afterwards. <br /><br />I did love the writing style. It's beautiful, provocative and interesting. From the very first page, I was hooked and I couldn't get enough of it. I'd read anything by this author now, because I absolutely love his writing style. I'd recommend this book to everyone for this reason, because my book basically looks like a rainbow because of everything I highlighted. That's saying a lot, since I almost never highlight in books. <br /><br />If you're ready to face all the drugs and sex in this book, I'd say go for it. The reading experience is awesome, even without a character to love.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10344152116346482196" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1177996521909659485" style="display:none">Crazy and ingenious. This story tells us how powerless we are in the hands of media sharks if we spend most of our time watching the box. <br />The author described very well of our modern world.<br />Aristotle once said that "a human is a society animal," he was definitely right. <br />The author tells via protagonist how miserable our society made by commercial producers or any other masters of PR. They make our choices, likes, and dislikes; they tell you what car to buy, they show you how to dress just to be a very close via various fashion magazines. They make for ya "Idols" which you should blindly follow. They show you modern technologies, which you should buy all the time, like the cell phones. <br />That's why every one of us who respects his or her life should turn off "the Box." And spend more time by reading basic and simple handbooks, and teach our kids to do the same. And show them that it is cooler to have a huge home library than any collection of X-Box and Sony PlayStation video games. I remember a wise quote from Denzel Washington's book -- children have never been superb at listening to their elders, but they have never failed to imitate them. </span>

<div id="review_1594852935" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1594852935" />
    <a title="Jos" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18102778-jos"><img alt="Jos" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365338097p2/18102778.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1594852935?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jos" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jos" href="/user/show/18102778-jos">Jos</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9783499233241" href="/book/show/1062387.Neununddrei_igneunzig_39_90">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/18102778-jos?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1594852935" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8753325660846584844">99 francs is Beigbeder's reckoning with the advertising industry. He shares his deeply cynical views on corporate life and ethics. The book is written as a meta fiction, i.e. the protagonist Octavo supposedly writes the book in an effort to be fired. He's fed up with his job, not enough to quit by himself but is happy to leave the decision to others, hoping for the golden handshake.<br/><br/>The content: cocaine, sexual perversions, corporate bullshit bingo, contempt for the mediocrity of business partner</span>
  <span id="freeText8753325660846584844" style="display:none">99 francs is Beigbeder's reckoning with the advertising industry. He shares his deeply cynical views on corporate life and ethics. The book is written as a meta fiction, i.e. the protagonist Octavo supposedly writes the book in an effort to be fired. He's fed up with his job, not enough to quit by himself but is happy to leave the decision to others, hoping for the golden handshake.<br /><br />The content: cocaine, sexual perversions, corporate bullshit bingo, contempt for the mediocrity of business partners, colleagues and customers. Inability to form relationships, childhood traumas, depressions, violence and suicides. Slogans correctly called claims in dozens.<br /><br />Beigbeder created the literary equivalent of fast-paced video clips. MTV aesthetics for the attention-deficit-disorder generation. Superficially, the advertisers with their self-concept as masters of the universe arouse envy. Beautiful people, status symbols aplenty, a lifestyle of parties, working with interesting creative guys on fancy projects. Behind the scenes, their lifes are hollow and aimless. Having everything and forming others' needs, nothing valuable is left. Excesses are the way out of the emptiness.<br /><br />I reread this book after I saw the movie on TV. The movie stays close to the book with only a few changes necessary to make it work as a film or to condense the plot into two hours. In direct comparison, the movie is the better format for this story. No real surprise given the topic. But the book is still very enjoyable for readers not minding occasional obscenity and can accept that business (and society) is run by a bunch of ruthless egoists.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8753325660846584844" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1432205436?book_show_action=true">Nov 03, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8909360-grigory?shelf=marketing">marketing</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8909360-grigory?shelf=fiction-france">fiction-france</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8909360-grigory?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1432205436" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9657877194766868217">The discourse part is a simplified and easy version of the leftist philosophers of the 60s and 70s (mostly Baudrillard, Marcuse, Fromm and such). It is not necessarily a bad thing since the originals are often verbose and require too much effort. The plot itself is quite predictable. On the whole it's a book to read and to forget. </span>

          </span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9789737075239" href="/book/show/13647923-13-99-lei">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer1173517677&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1173517677" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer470527070224586318">If I had still been in high school I might have appreciated it more, but wouldn't understand it. I'm not sure I've grasped it all, now, but hey?! <br/><br/>LIKED<br/>- The package. Writing technique and architecture. Putting myself in all those instances was great and new. I only realized that after the first two parts, so I would've liked more emphasis on it. Sometimes, I even wanted to know more about the "YOUs"... (Who are they?!) But I always enjoyed multilateral perspectives in any form and this made the </span>
  <span id="freeText470527070224586318" style="display:none">If I had still been in high school I might have appreciated it more, but wouldn't understand it. I'm not sure I've grasped it all, now, but hey?! <br /><br />LIKED<br />- The package. Writing technique and architecture. Putting myself in all those instances was great and new. I only realized that after the first two parts, so I would've liked more emphasis on it. Sometimes, I even wanted to know more about the "YOUs"... (Who are they?!) But I always enjoyed multilateral perspectives in any form and this made the entire picture seem easier to paint. <br />- LOVED THE "COMMERCIALS" BETWEEN THE CHAPTERS<br />- The advertising creativity. <br />- The outrageous facts/truths of how media drives our incentives. <br />- Some of the ideas - both spiritually and pragmatic ones. Thought provoking ones.<br />- The somewhat optimistic end with that decent world. <br /><br />DISLIKED<br />- Language - sometimes it was quite gross, but that's just because I'm not into that kind of language. <br />- At some point, 3 of the characters, kill an old lady. I found that beyond disgusting, unrealistic and entirely unfair towards the quality of the novel. (The lack of remorse was adding to the above mentioned) - Until they got their punishment, I almost felt like a desperate (girl)friend wanting to scold them. But this was a cowardly attempt of expressing frustration. I would've expected something a lot more intelligent from him.<br />- Sometimes, the dialogues would take me out of the story completely. Should have worked on integrating them better. <br />- Started pretty cool, but when he went into the "love thing", it all got pretty boring (FOR ME), especially because he's got the same love/lover pattern as in Love Lasts Three Years. I felt like professionally he was in charge, but personally it was only his partner that described him - like he would make his career, but would have no personality in the actual "love thing". Plus, I am really tired of all that "I'm-messing-up-my-life-because-I'm-all-frustrated" character. <br /><br /><br />Don't really think I'll read something of his again. Unless he drops the lows and only focuses on the highly and respectable notes of his characters. I would like to see a character who is interesting and complex because of his positive traits and not his drama/decadence/degradation. <br /><br /><br />NOT DONE! TO ADD MORE!</span>
  <a data-text-id="470527070224586318" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer15530402530048372460">I have tried at other French books, but most french literature is way over intellectualized and thus falls short of actually being intellectual. 99F is intellectualized, but in this sleazy modern way. It is about a guy who is riding high on the horse as an advertising executive and has pretty much everything anyone could want in our modern society, and this ultimately throws him into despair. A poetic and cynical look at modern consumerism. People in France generally either hate this guy and his</span>
  <span id="freeText15530402530048372460" style="display:none">I have tried at other French books, but most french literature is way over intellectualized and thus falls short of actually being intellectual. 99F is intellectualized, but in this sleazy modern way. It is about a guy who is riding high on the horse as an advertising executive and has pretty much everything anyone could want in our modern society, and this ultimately throws him into despair. A poetic and cynical look at modern consumerism. People in France generally either hate this guy and his books, or love him. I wish it had an English translation. It might but I haven't found one. </span>

              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16021344157633421560">I didn't really like this that much. It had clever moments, but it just wasn't there in a way I'd like it to be, it sort of straddled Brett Easton Ellis, Irvine Welsh and Michel Houellebecq (oo er!), but not emulating any of them well enough to be a really good read. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3195148250" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer14052647775517743499">What i liked: <br/>1. That the main character is not built to make you as a reader empathise with him, but even despise him. I think this offers a different experience to the reader which is interesting even if uncomfortable.<br/>2. That at the end he cannot accept reality (and i believe this could be partly because he always thought he could have what he wanted eventually, as soon as he would actually pursue it).<br/><br/>What i didn't like:<br/>1. Subtly but consistently he implies that his personal issues (i.e. terri</span>
  <span id="freeText14052647775517743499" style="display:none">What i liked: <br />1. That the main character is not built to make you as a reader empathise with him, but even despise him. I think this offers a different experience to the reader which is interesting even if uncomfortable.<br />2. That at the end he cannot accept reality (and i believe this could be partly because he always thought he could have what he wanted eventually, as soon as he would actually pursue it).<br /><br />What i didn't like:<br />1. Subtly but consistently he implies that his personal issues (i.e. terrified of intimacy, of responsibility over another person's life, always fantasising the strong exerting power over the weak - even though rationally he is against this on a large scale, etc.) are a result of the industry he's part of; and the fact that he doesn't address this external blaming at all throughout the book makes me suspect it's not a fault of the character but of the author [i'd like to have missed something here though...].<br />2. The beginning of the book is very misleading in making you understand the book will be an insight into the advertising industry and it's effect on the innocent consumer, whereas you discover very early on that he is an unreliable narrator, that this is a personal story and his actual arguments against consumerism and manipulation are some ridiculously simplistic facts about opulence and poverty, put next to each other. Even if you agree with his main idea, his puerile way of making his point weakens his case.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14052647775517743499" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

Beigbeder, who used to work as a publicitary agent, wrote a vehement attack on the world of billboards, catchy slogans and consumerism. And what's even more - it's pretty damn funny.<br/><br/>We follow the adventures of a publicity hotshot called Octave, who is writing down his story in an attempt to get fired from his job. Everything he does to get himself fired backfires though, resulting in a promotion, big bonuses and bigger contracts. It's a world where vile sloga</span>
  <span id="freeText15754664500792997906" style="display:none">'99 francs' is a very enticing read. Beigbeder, who used to work as a publicitary agent, wrote a vehement attack on the world of billboards, catchy slogans and consumerism. And what's even more - it's pretty damn funny.<br /><br />We follow the adventures of a publicity hotshot called Octave, who is writing down his story in an attempt to get fired from his job. Everything he does to get himself fired backfires though, resulting in a promotion, big bonuses and bigger contracts. It's a world where vile slogans get rewarded, where manipulation is key to succes and, more importantly, the world we all live in today. There's also a more romantic side to the story. Octave is trying to escape. He doesn't really know how he is supposed to go about that, but still. There's some really nice French in here. This book is dangerous, but essential.<br /><br />Beigbeder is a writer pur sang - go check it out.</span>

  <span id="freeText7148976291688081349" style="display:none">While I get what he is trying to say, I barely made myself finish this book. Some of the passages are so graphic I skipped over them altogether. The book is supposed to make you realize that you are a sheep, controlled by the admen, but you know what? Maybe I don't like to be told that I am a sheep. And maybe excessive use of the word "cocaine" is really not a necessary tactic to make me realize that the main character is a user. I read that the author himself recently got arrested for possession of this drug, and the novel generally reads like he wrote a draft under the influence and never bothered to reread or edit it in any way. </span>
  <a data-text-id="7148976291688081349" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('AM1gWTHnHAVAPNGiGZttsgqXeeu7fVs+/q4fXe2qi0qut8qPacpTTWxpuUSpKqDiR9Le0OeLtDu7Muw52nrjgw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('1zEOfwOKLQ52tWP8t33zTbDIp/znHxl7q7bofpfITox5S6SpW6diRlrgCxoHzD4d/Y0Ax7vp9n7uKhsaoBgmRQ==')})
  }
//]]>

Amoureuse folle, Hell va devoir endurer &quot;ses discours nihilistes et son inégalable perversité&quot;, c\'est-à-dire découvrir l\'amour, cet autre enfer.  Pour son premier roman, Lolita Pille, qui n\'a que 19 ans, n\'évite pas toujours le piège de la caricature et de la facilité. C\'est de son âge. <div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer12539560752873075325">Beigbeder was born into a privileged family in Neuilly-sur-Seine, France. His mother, Christine de Chasteigner, is a translator of mawkish novels (Barbara Cartland et al.); his father, Jean-Michel Beigbeder, is a headhunter. He studied at the Lycée Montaigne and Louis-le-Grand, and later at the Institut d'Etudes Politiques de Paris. Upon graduation at the at the age of 24, began work as an adverti</span>
  <span id="freeText12539560752873075325" style="display:none">Beigbeder was born into a privileged family in Neuilly-sur-Seine, France. His mother, Christine de Chasteigner, is a translator of mawkish novels (Barbara Cartland et al.); his father, Jean-Michel Beigbeder, is a headhunter. He studied at the Lycée Montaigne and Louis-le-Grand, and later at the Institut d'Etudes Politiques de Paris. Upon graduation at the at the age of 24, began work as an advertising executive, author, broadcaster, publisher, and dilettante.<br /><br />In 1994, Beigbeder founded the "Prix de Flore", which takes its name from the famous and plush Café de Flore in Saint-Germain-des-Prés. The prize is awarded annually to a promising young French author. Vincent Ravalec, Jacques A. Bertrand, Michel Houellebecq are among those who have won the prize. In 2004, the tenth anniversary of the prize, it was awarded to the only American to ever receive it, Bruce Benderson. Two of Beigbeder's novels, <i>99 Francs</i> (Jan Kounen, 2007) and <i>L'amour dure trois ans</i> (Beigbeder, 2011), have been adapted for the cinema.<br /><br />In 2002, he presented the TV talk show "Hypershow" on French channel Canal+, co-presented with Jonathan Lambert, Sabine Crossen and Henda. That year he also advised French Communist Party candidate Robert Hue in the presidential election.<br /><br />He worked for a few years as a publisher for Flammarion. He left Flammarion in 2006.<br /><br />In May 2007 he spent time in the United States to shoot a film about the reclusive American author, J.D. Salinger.</span>
